        <meta name="description" content="The sweet cherry (Prunus avium) is one of the most economically important fruit species in the world. However, there is a limited amount of genetic information available for this species, which hinders breeding efforts at a molecular level. We were able to describe a high-quality reference genome assembly and annotation of the diploid sweet cherry (2n = 2x = 16) cv. Tieton using linked-read sequencing technology. We generated over 750 million clean reads, representing 112.63 GB of raw sequencing data. The Supernova assembler produced a more highly-ordered and continuous genome sequence than the current P. avium draft genome, with a contig N50 of 63.65 KB and a scaffold N50 of 2.48 MB. The final scaffold assembly was 280.33 MB in length, representing 82.12% of the estimated Tieton genome. Eight chromosome-scale pseudomolecules were constructed, completing a 214 MB sequence of the final scaffold assembly. De novo, homology-based, and RNA-seq methods were used together to predict 30,975 protein-coding loci. 98.39% of core eukaryotic genes and 97.43% of single copy orthologues were identified in the embryo plant, indicating the completeness of the assembly. Linked-read sequencing technology was effective in constructing a high-quality reference genome of the sweet cherry, which will benefit the molecular breeding and cultivar identification in this species.">

        <meta name="twitter:description" content="The sweet cherry (Prunus avium) is one of the most economically important fruit species in the world. However, there is a limited amount of genetic information available for this species, which hinders breeding efforts at a molecular level. We were able to describe a high-quality reference genome assembly and annotation of the diploid sweet cherry (2n = 2x = 16) cv. Tieton using linked-read sequencing technology. We generated over 750 million clean reads, representing 112.63 GB of raw sequencing data. The Supernova assembler produced a more highly-ordered and continuous genome sequence than the current P. avium draft genome, with a contig N50 of 63.65 KB and a scaffold N50 of 2.48 MB. The final scaffold assembly was 280.33 MB in length, representing 82.12% of the estimated Tieton genome. Eight chromosome-scale pseudomolecules were constructed, completing a 214 MB sequence of the final scaffold assembly. De novo, homology-based, and RNA-seq methods were used together to predict 30,975 protein-coding loci. 98.39% of core eukaryotic genes and 97.43% of single copy orthologues were identified in the embryo plant, indicating the completeness of the assembly. Linked-read sequencing technology was effective in constructing a high-quality reference genome of the sweet cherry, which will benefit the molecular breeding and cultivar identification in this species.">

<script>
    var campaign_keywords = ['utm_source', 'utm_medium', 'utm_campaign', 'utm_content', 'utm_term'];
    var kw = '';
    var lastUtms = {};
    var firstUtms = {};
    var allUtms = {};

    function campaignParams() {
        var index;
        for (index = 0; index < campaign_keywords.length; ++index) {
            kw = getQueryParam(document.URL, campaign_keywords[index]);
            if (kw.length) {
                lastUtms[campaign_keywords[index] + '-last'] = kw;
                firstUtms[campaign_keywords[index] + '-first'] = kw;
                allUtms[campaign_keywords[index] + '-all'] = kw;
            }
        }
    }

    function updatePreregCookie(preregCookie, firstUtmKey) {
        var utmVal = firstUtms[firstUtmKey];
        if (utmVal) {
            var existingPreregCampaign = $.cookie(preregCookie);
            var appendPreregCampaign;
            if (!existingPreregCampaign) {
                appendPreregCampaign = utmVal;
            } else {
                appendPreregCampaign = existingPreregCampaign + ',' + utmVal;

            }
            $.cookie(preregCookie, appendPreregCampaign, {expires: 365, path: "/"});
        }
    }

    function getQueryParam(url, param) {
        // Expects a raw URL
        param = param.replace(/[[]/, "\[").replace(/[]]/, "\]");
        var regexS = "[\?&]" + param + "=([^&#]*)",
                regex = new RegExp( regexS ),
                results = regex.exec(url);
        if (results === null || (results && typeof(results[1]) !== 'string' && results[1].length)) {
            return '';
        } else {
            return decodeURIComponent(results[1]).replace(/\W/gi, ' ');
        }
    }

    function articlePageEvent() {
        var articleContainer = $('.publication-jsondata');
        if (articleContainer.length) {
            var data = articleContainer.data('publication-meta');

            // Must be public
            if (data.publicationSubjects.length) {

                var eventName = 'Viewed-article';
                var preprint = data.preprint;
                if (preprint) {
                    eventName = 'Viewed-preprint';
                }

                data['ip-hash'] = 'b840d58e443ff19b92460a9e03ca728f';
                mixpanel.track(eventName, data);
            }
        }
    }

    function sectionListViewEvent() {
            }
</script>

<div>
<h2>Abstract</h2>
<div class="abstract" itemprop="description">
<p>The sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) is one of the most economically important fruit species in the world. However, there is a limited amount of genetic information available for this species, which hinders breeding efforts at a molecular level. We were able to describe a high-quality reference genome assembly and annotation of the diploid sweet cherry (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 16) cv. Tieton using linked-read sequencing technology. We generated over 750 million clean reads, representing 112.63 GB of raw sequencing data. The Supernova assembler produced a more highly-ordered and continuous genome sequence than the current <i>P. avium</i> draft genome, with a contig N50 of 63.65 KB and a scaffold N50 of 2.48 MB. The final scaffold assembly was 280.33 MB in length, representing 82.12% of the estimated Tieton genome. Eight chromosome-scale pseudomolecules were constructed, completing a 214 MB sequence of the final scaffold assembly. De novo, homology-based, and RNA-seq methods were used together to predict 30,975 protein-coding loci. 98.39% of core eukaryotic genes and 97.43% of single copy orthologues were identified in the embryo plant, indicating the completeness of the assembly. Linked-read sequencing technology was effective in constructing a high-quality reference genome of the sweet cherry, which will benefit the molecular breeding and cultivar identification in this species.</p>
</div>
</div></header><main><div class="body" lang="en">
<section class="sec" id="intro">
<h2 class="heading">Introduction</h2>
<p id="p-1">The sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>), originated in Asia Minor near the Black Sea and the Caspian Sea. It is known as one of the most economically significant fruit species in the world (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Cherries:%20botany,%20production%20and%20uses&amp;author=Quero-Garc%C3%ADa&amp;publication_year=2017" title="Cherries: botany, production and uses" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-38">Quero-García et al., 2017</a>) and its production in China has increased dramatically over the last three decades with the expansion of acreage dedicated to its cultivation. Recent breeding efforts have focused on improving yield, fruit quality, tree architecture and biotic and abiotic resistance (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0140-8" title="Prunus genetics and applications after de novo genome sequencing: achievements and prospects" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-1">Aranzana et al., 2019</a>). Sweet cherry and other <i>Prunus</i> crops have a long juvenile period, which means that traditional breeding methods are slow to produce improvements (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Cherries:%20botany,%20production%20and%20uses&amp;author=Quero-Garc%C3%ADa&amp;publication_year=2017" title="Cherries: botany, production and uses" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-38">Quero-García et al., 2017</a>). Marker-assisted breeding and genomic selection can speed up the breeding cycle, but these methods require a high-quality reference genome in order to obtain a sufficient amount of genetic variants and to identify the regulatory regions controlling the morphological and physiological characteristics of the plant (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0140-8" title="Prunus genetics and applications after de novo genome sequencing: achievements and prospects" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-1">Aranzana et al., 2019</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs11295-015-0834-5" title="Current applications, challenges, and perspectives of marker-assisted seedling selection in Rosaceae tree fruit breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-40">Ru et al., 2015</a>). Only one draft genome assembly of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>) and one mitochondrial genome sequence of cv. Summit have been reported (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1080%2F23802359.2019.1617082" title="The complete mitochondrial genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium cv. ‘summit’)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-57">Yan et al., 2019</a>), despite the simple genome of the sweet cherry (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 16). The draft genome of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki was sequenced using Illumina short-read sequencing technology, resulting in a fragmented assembly of 272.4 MB with a scaffold N50 of 219.6 KB (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>). The linked-read sequencing pipeline developed by 10× Genomics may result in more continuous genomes for the sweet cherry at a lower financial cost (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fhmg%2Fddy177" title="Long reads: their purpose and place" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-37">Pollard et al., 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnbt.3432" title="Haplotyping germline and cancer genomes with high-throughput linked-read sequencing" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-59">Zheng et al., 2016</a>). This technology use a barcoded sequencing library to generate long-range information (preferably &gt;100 KB) and standard short-read sequencing to ensure massive throughput and high accuracy. It was designed for human genome assembly, but has been used effectively in many other animal and plant species, including the wild dog, proso millet pepper and soybean (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fgigascience%2Fgiy124" title="Cost-effective assembly of the African wild dog (Lycaon pictus) genome using linked reads" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-2">Armstrong et al., 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fs41438-017-0011-0" title="Reference quality assembly of the 3.5-Gb genome of Capsicum annuum from a single linked-read library" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-21">Hulse-Kemp et al., 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Ftpj.13931" title="Assembly and annotation of a draft genome sequence for Glycine latifolia, a perennial wild relative of soybean" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-29">Liu et al., 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5040-z" title="Linked read technology for assembling large complex and polyploid genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-34">Ott et al., 2018</a>).</p>
<p id="p-2">We demonstrated that linked-read technology is effective in the de novo assembly of the genome of the sweet cheery cv. Tieton, which is the most popular cherry variety in China. The sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly surpasses the cv. Satonishiki genome assembled using Illumina short-reads in continuity, with a tenfold improvement of scaffold N50 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>). The high-quality genome assembly and annotation in this study are valuable for genetic marker development and gene mapping, which may improve sweet cherry breeding. Our assembly platform will support future de novo genome assemblies for other <i>Prunus</i> crops using the linked reads method.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec" id="materials|methods">
<h2 class="heading">Materials and Methods</h2>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Sample and DNA extraction</h3>
<p id="p-3">Leaf samples were collected from the sweet cherry cv. Tieton grown in the experimental orchard of Shandong Institute of Pomology, Taian, Shandong Province, China, and frozen in liquid nitrogen. High-molecular-weight (HMW) genomic DNA (gDNA) was extracted from the frozen leaves using MagAttract HMW DNA Kit (Qiagen, Hilden, Germany) following the manufacturer’s protocol. The gDNA was quantified using Implen NanoPhotometer P330 (Implen, Munich, Germany) and assessed using agarose gel electrophoresis.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Chromium library construction and sequencing</h3>
<p id="p-4">The single Chromium library was constructed by CapitalBio Technology Inc. (Beijing, China) using the purified HMW gDNA sample. The library was sequenced in one lane as 150 nt-Chromium-linked paired-end reads on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten sequencer (Illumina, <a class="uri" href="http://www.illumina.com/">http://www.illumina.com/</a>). We filtered out raw reads with &gt;5% undetermined bases (Ns), &gt;30% nucleotides quality score lower than 20, and the adapter sequence overlap &gt;5 bp.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">De novo assembly and evaluation</h3>
<p id="p-5">We estimated the size of the sweet cherry genome based on the <i>k</i>-mer frequency of the sequence data using the <i>k</i>-mer counting program Jellyfish (v.2.0.8) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtr011" title="A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-32">Marcais &amp; Kingsford, 2011</a>) and GenomeScope (v1.0.0) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtx153" title="GenomeScope: fast reference-free genome profiling from short reads" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-50">Vurture et al., 2017</a>). The genome was assembled and scaffolded using the Supernova assembler (v2.0, <a class="uri" href="https://www.10xgenomics.com/">https://www.10xgenomics.com/</a>). This program links sequencing reads to the originating HMW DNA molecule using barcoded information and constructs phased, whole-genome de novo assemblies form the Chromium-prepared library (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.214874.116" title="Direct determination of diploid genome sequences" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-53">Weisenfeld et al., 2017</a>). Chromium-linked reads of different sizes (40×, 50×, 60×, 65×, 68×, 70× and 75×) were used as input data. The assembly, using 70× coverage of the reads, was selected for analysis based on superior quality, and higher contig N50 and scaffold N50. Default parameters were set and two pseudohap assemblies were generated; pseudohap1 was used for further analysis. A total of 150 million reads were sampled and aligned to the assembled genome sequence; the quality of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly was evaluated using the Burrows–Wheller Alignment tool (BWA, 0.7.17-r1188) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtp324" title="Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-27">Li &amp; Durbin, 2009</a>). Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach (CEGMA, v2.5) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtm071" title="CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-35">Parra, Bradnam &amp; Korf, 2007</a>) and Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO, v3.0, embryophyta_odb10) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtv351" title="BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-42">Simao et al., 2015</a>) were used to assess the completeness of the assembly.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Chromosome-scale pseudomolecule construction</h3>
<p id="p-6">Scaffolds were assembled using the Supernova assembler and were ordered and oriented using seven previously published sweet cherry genetic maps for the construction of the chromosome-scale pseudomolecules. Five of the seven maps were built by <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al. (2017)</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0048305" title="Development and evaluation of a genome-wide 6K SNP array for diploid sweet cherry and tetraploid sour cherry" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-36">Peace et al. (2012)</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0054743" title="Construction and comparative analyses of highly dense linkage maps of two sweet cherry intra-specific progenies of commercial cultivars" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Klagges et al. (2013)</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs11295-018-1252-2" title="High-density linkage maps constructed in sweet cherry (Prunus avium L.) using cross- and self-pollination populations reveal chromosomal homozygosity in inbred families and non-syntenic regions with the peach genome" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-7">Calle et al. (2018)</a> and <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0127750" title="Construction of high density sweet cherry (Prunus avium L.) linkage maps using microsatellite markers and SNPs detected by genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-18">Guajardo et al. (2015)</a>. The initials of the first author were used to name their respective maps and the maps are referred to as KS, CP, CK, AC and VG. The other two maps, named JWF (the framework map of the WxL map) and JWF1 (the second round of the WxL map), were both reported by <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0141261" title="Construction of commercial sweet cherry linkage maps and QTL analysis for trunk diameter" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-51">Wang et al. (2015)</a>. Genetic markers and/or flanking sequences for these maps were aligned to the current scaffolds using GMAP (v2018-07-04) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbti310" title="GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-55">Wu &amp; Watanabe, 2005</a>) as described by <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fs41438-017-0011-0" title="Reference quality assembly of the 3.5-Gb genome of Capsicum annuum from a single linked-read library" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-21">Hulse-Kemp et al. (2018)</a>. Markers were manually filtered out if they were aligned to more than one scaffold or the same scaffold in different linkage groups. The alignment results of GMAP were fitted into ALLMAPS (v0.8.4)(<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0573-1" title="ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-46">Tang et al., 2015</a>) to generate the final consensus map and chromosome-scale pseudomolecules. Different weight parameters were tried for the seven linkage maps and the optimal weight settings with the largest number of anchored and oriented scaffolds were: KS = 2, CP = 3, CK = 1, AC = 1, VG = 1, JWF = 1 and JWF1 = 1.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Identification of repetitive elements in sweet cherry genome</h3>
<p id="p-7">Homology-based and de novo methods were combined to identify repetitive and transposon elements in our final assembly using RepeatMasker (v.4.0.6) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://www.repeatmasker.org" title="RepeatMasker Open-4.0.6" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-45">Smit, Hubley &amp; Green, 2016</a>) and RepeatModeler (v.1.0.11) (<a class="uri" href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">RNA-seq analysis</h3>
<p id="p-8">Total RNA was extracted from the young leaves of a single plant for genome sequencing. The cDNA library was constructed based on the description of <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0121164" title="Comparative transcriptome analysis of genes involved in anthocyanin biosynthesis in the red and yellow fruits of sweet cherry (Prunus avium L.)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-52">Wei et al. (2015)</a> and sequenced by CapitalBio Technology Inc. (Beijing, China) using the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. The adapters were trimmed and low-quality reads were removed before the remaining high quality reads were assembled by Trinity (v2.8.5) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnbt.1883" title="Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-16">Grabherr et al., 2011</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Non-coding RNA prediction, protein-coding gene prediction and functional annotation</h3>
<p id="p-9">INFERNAL (v1.1.2) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtp157" title="Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-33">Nawrocki, Kolbe &amp; Eddy, 2009</a>) was used to identify the non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs) in the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome against the RFAM database (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2Fgki081" title="Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-17">Griffiths-Jones et al., 2005</a>). The tRNAs were identified by tRNAscan-SE (v2.0.5) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2F25.5.955" title="tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-30">Lowe &amp; Eddy, 1997</a>). The rRNAs were identified using RNAmmer (v1.1.2) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2Fgkm160" title="RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-26">Lagesen et al., 2007</a>).</p>
<p id="p-10">Homology-based, de novo and RNA-seq methods were combined to predict the protein-coding genes in sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome. Augustus (v3.3.2) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtr010" title="A novel hybrid gene prediction method employing protein multiple sequence alignments" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-23">Keller et al., 2011</a>) and SNAP (v2013-11-29) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2105-5-59" title="Gene finding in novel genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-25">Korf, 2004</a>) were used in the de novo annotation to predict the protein-coding gene in repeat-masked genome sequences. The predicted genes were annotated by Genewise (v2.4.1) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.1865504" title="Genewise and genomewise" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-5">Birney, Clamp &amp; Durbin, 2004</a>) and Exonerate (v2.4.0) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2105-6-31" title="Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-43">Slater &amp; Birney, 2005</a>). The Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments (PASA, v2.4.1) pipeline (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2Fgkg770" title="Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-19">Haas et al., 2003</a>) was used in transcriptome-assistant method with the unigenes assembled by the RNA-seq data. EVidenceModeler (EVM, v1.1.1) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fgb-2008-9-1-r7" title="Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-20">Haas et al., 2008</a>) and PASA were used to combine the predicted results.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Gene family analysis</h3>
<p id="p-11">OrthoFinder (v2.2.7) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs13059-015-0721-2" title="OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-13">Emms &amp; Kelly, 2015</a>) was used to identify the orthologous genes from 13 plant genomes of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton (<i>Prunus avium</i>, Pa), peach (<i>Prunus persica</i>, Pp), Chinese plum (<i>Prunus mume</i>, Pm), flowering cherry (<i>Prunus yedoensis</i>, Py), apple (<i>Malus x domestica</i>, Md), pear (<i>Pyrus bretschneideri</i>, Pb), black raspberry (<i>Rubus occidentalis</i>, Ro), strawberry (<i>Fragaria vesca</i>, Fv), rose (<i>Rosa chinensis</i>, Rc), orange (<i>Citrus sinensis</i>, Cs), grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>, Vv), tomato (<i>Solanum lyconpersicum</i>, Sl), and arabidopsis (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, At) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature11119" title="The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-47">The Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fncomms2290" title="The genome of Prunus mume" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-58">Zhang et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=The%20genome%20of%20the%20pear%20(Pyrus%20bretschneideri%20Rehd.)&amp;author=Wu&amp;publication_year=2013" title="The genome of the pear (Pyrus bretschneideri Rehd.)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-54">Wu et al., 2013</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fng.2472" title="The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-56">Xu et al., 2013</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.gdata.2017.09.002" title="A new version of the grapevine reference genome assembly (12X.v2) and of its annotation (VCost.v3)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-9">Canaguier et al., 2017</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fng.3886" title="High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-10">Daccord et al., 2017</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Genome%20re-annotation%20of%20the%20wild%20strawberry%20Fragaria%20vesca%20using%20extensive%20Illumina-%20and%20SMRT-based%20RNA-seq%20datasets&amp;author=Li&amp;publication_year=2017" title="Genome re-annotation of the wild strawberry Fragaria vesca using extensive Illumina- and SMRT-based RNA-seq datasets" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-28">Li et al., 2017</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs12864-017-3606-9" title="The Peach v2.0 release: high-resolution linkage mapping and deep resequencing improve chromosome-scale assembly and contiguity" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-49">Verde et al., 2017</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs13059-018-1497-y" title="Draft genome sequence of wild Prunus yedoensis reveals massive inter-specific hybridization between sympatric flowering cherries" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-4">Baek et al., 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0110-3" title="The Rosa genome provides new insights into the domestication of modern roses" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-39">Raymond et al., 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1105%2Ftpc.18.00024" title="Correction of persistent errors in arabidopsis reference mitochondrial genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-44">Sloan, Wu &amp; Sharbrough, 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fgigascience%2Fgiy094" title="A near complete, chromosome-scale assembly of the black raspberry (Rubus occidentalis) genome" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">VanBuren et al., 2018</a>). The protein sequences of each plant genome were generated from their most recently annotated versions and were used as input sequences for OrthoFinder. <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-2" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-2">Table S1</a> shows the annotated version and reference of the other 12 plant genomes except for our sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome. CAFÉ (v4.2) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtl097" title="CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-11">De Bie et al., 2006</a>) was used to analyze the expansion and contraction of their gene families. The species tree was generated using STRIDE (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fmolbev%2Fmsx259" title="STRIDE: species tree root inference from gene duplication events" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-14">Emms &amp; Kelly, 2017</a>), as part of OrthoFinder and used as the input phylogenetic tree for CAFÉ.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Comparison between sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome and cv. Satonishiki genome</h3>
<p id="p-12">D-GENIES (v1.2.0) was used to compare the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome with the cv. Satonishiki genome (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.7717%2Fpeerj.4958" title="D-GENIES: dot plot large genomes in an interactive, efficient and simple way" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-6">Cabanettes &amp; Klopp, 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>). The whole sequence synteny analysis of the two assemblies were compared in both scaffold level and pseudochromosome level.</p>
<p id="p-13">To compare the gene content between the two genome assemblies, we used three annotation versions that are the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome annotation, the cv. Satonishiki genome annotation (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>), and an improved and re-annotated assembly of cv. Satonishiki genome released by NCBI Eukaryotic Genome Annotation Pipeline (NCBI <i>Prunus avium</i> Annotation Release 100, <a class="uri" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genome/annotation_euk/Prunus_avium/100/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genome/annotation_euk/Prunus_avium/100/</a>). OrthoFinder was used to compare the gene content among the three annotations (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs13059-015-0721-2" title="OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-13">Emms &amp; Kelly, 2015</a>).</p>
</section>
</section>
<section class="sec" id="results|discussion">
<h2 class="heading">Results and Discussion</h2>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Sequencing summary</h3>
<p id="p-14">For sweet cherry cv. Tieton, a total of 121.61 GB of raw sequencing data was generated with more than 810 million Chromium-linked paired-end reads. <a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-1" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-1">Table 1</a> shows the statistics of the sequencing for the linked-read library. The low quality reads were filtered out and 750,890,534 clean reads were used for de novo assembly. The average Q20 was 97.52% and GC content was 40.8%. A cDNA library was constructed and sequenced to improve the precision of the genome annotation. As shown in <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-3" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-3">Table S2</a>, over 78 million 150-nt length paired-end reads were generated and assembled.</p>

</figure>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Determination of genome size and heterozygosity</h3>
<p id="p-15">The genome size of sweet cherry cv. Tieton was estimated to be 341.38 MB based on 37-nt <i>k</i>-mer, which is very close to the genome size of 338 MB estimated by flow cytometry (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2FBF02672069" title="Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-3">Arumuganathan &amp; Earle, 1991</a>). The <i>k</i>-mer distribution generated by GenomeScope was shown in <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Fig. S1</a>. The sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome estimated by <i>k</i>-mer method was 352.9 MB (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>), larger than cv. Tieton genome. The genome size difference is probably due to the variety difference, but also may be caused by different library construction and sequencing methods. Heterozygosity of sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome was estimated to be 0.45%, and the repeat content was estimated to be 48.5% as shown in <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Fig. S1</a>.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Genome assembly and quality-assessment</h3>
<p id="p-16">The Supernova assembler (version 2.0) was used in de novo assembly and different sizes (40×, 50×, 60×, 65×, 68×, 70×, and 75×) of the Chromium-linked reads were attempted (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.214874.116" title="Direct determination of diploid genome sequences" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-53">Weisenfeld et al., 2017</a>). <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-4" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-4">Table S3</a> listed these assembly results, illustrating that the assembly using 70× coverage reads has the best assembly quality, and was selected for following analyses. GapCloser filled gaps in the raw sequencing data (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F2047-217X-1-18" title="SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-31">Luo et al., 2012</a>), resulting in the draft genome assembly of sweet cherry cv. Tieton of 280.33 MB with contig N50 and scaffold N50 sizes of 63.65 KB and 2.48 MB, respectively. Our sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly had tenfold better contiguity than the cv. Satonishiki genome assembly (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>). The whole assembly increased in size from 272.36 to 280.33 MB, whereas scaffold N50 increased from 219 KB to 2.48 MB (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-2" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-2">Table 2</a>).</p>
<figure class="table-wrap" id="table-2"><div class="caption">
<span class="caption-label">Table 2: </span>

</div>

</div>
<div class="fn" id="table-2fn1" data-jats-fn-type="other">
<p id="p-18">Mb, Megabase; kb, Kilobase; GC, Guanine-cytosine; Ns, Ambiguous bases.</p>
</div>
</div>
</figure>
<p id="p-19">A total of 150 million reads were sampled and 99.02% of the sampled reads were aligned to the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome sequence using BWA (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtp324" title="Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-27">Li &amp; Durbin, 2009</a>), shown in <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-5" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-5">Table S4</a>. CEGMA (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtm071" title="CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-35">Parra, Bradnam &amp; Korf, 2007</a>) and BUSCO (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtv351" title="BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-42">Simao et al., 2015</a>) were used to evaluate the completeness of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome and results were summarized in <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-6" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-6">Table S5</a>. Out of 248 core eukaryotic genes, 231 and 13 were found to be complete and partial genes in the CEGMA assessment, respectively. BUSCO analysis showed that our assembly captured 1,403 (97.43%) of the 1,440 single-copy orthologous genes of the embryo plant, of which 1,381 (95.9%) were complete (1,345 single-copy and 36 duplicated-copy), showing that the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly is well covered the gene space of the sweet cherry genome.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Chromosome-scale pseudomolecule construction</h3>
<p id="p-20">A consensus map was constructed from previously reported sweet cherry genetic maps for the chromosome-scale pseudomolecule construction (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs11295-018-1252-2" title="High-density linkage maps constructed in sweet cherry (Prunus avium L.) using cross- and self-pollination populations reveal chromosomal homozygosity in inbred families and non-syntenic regions with the peach genome" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-7">Calle et al., 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0127750" title="Construction of high density sweet cherry (Prunus avium L.) linkage maps using microsatellite markers and SNPs detected by genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-18">Guajardo et al., 2015</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0054743" title="Construction and comparative analyses of highly dense linkage maps of two sweet cherry intra-specific progenies of commercial cultivars" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Klagges et al., 2013</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0048305" title="Development and evaluation of a genome-wide 6K SNP array for diploid sweet cherry and tetraploid sour cherry" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-36">Peace et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0141261" title="Construction of commercial sweet cherry linkage maps and QTL analysis for trunk diameter" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-51">Wang et al., 2015</a>). GMAP (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbti310" title="GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-55">Wu &amp; Watanabe, 2005</a>) and ALLMAPS (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs13059-014-0573-1" title="ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-46">Tang et al., 2015</a>) were used to organize scaffolds onto eight chromosome-scale pseudomolecules (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fs41438-017-0011-0" title="Reference quality assembly of the 3.5-Gb genome of Capsicum annuum from a single linked-read library" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-21">Hulse-Kemp et al., 2018</a>). A total of 494 scaffolds representing more than 214 MB sequences, were anchored to eight chromosome-scale pseudomolecules of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome using 7,838 genetic markers (36.6 markers per Mb). 202.6 of the 214 MB anchored sequences were oriented, the anchor rate and synteny of the maps were shown in <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-7" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-7">Table S6</a> and <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-1" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-1">Fig. 1</a>. This formation resulted in a higher contiguity than the sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome, consisting of 905 scaffolds spanning 191.7 MB (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig" itemprop="image" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject" id="fig-1"><div class="image-container"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1-2x.jpg" title="View the full image" class="fresco" data-fresco-caption="Figure 1: Pseudomolecule construction of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) by assigning scaffolds to seven genetic maps." data-fresco-group="figure" data-fresco-options="fit: 'width', ui: 'outside', thumbnails: false, loop: true, position: true, overflow: true, preload: false"><img class="graphic" src="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1-1x.jpg" itemprop="contentUrl" sizes="(min-width: 1200px) 581px, (max-width: 1199px) and (min-width: 980px) 462px, (max-width: 979px) and (min-width: 768px) 347px, (max-width: 767px) calc(100vw - 50px)" srcset="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1-2x.jpg 1200w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1-1x.jpg 600w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1-small.jpg 355w" data-image-id="fig-1" alt="Pseudomolecule construction of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) by assigning scaffolds to seven genetic maps." data-full="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1-full.png" data-thumb="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1-thumb.jpg" data-original="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1.png" data-image-type="figure" data-jats-mimetype="image" data-jats-mime-subtype="png" width="600" height="478"></a></div>
<figcaption itemprop="description">
<h4 class="heading">
<span class="caption-label">Figure 1: </span>Pseudomolecule construction of sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) by assigning scaffolds to seven genetic maps.</h4>
<span class="p">Chr 1–8 represents constructed pseudomolecules by merging seven genetic maps. AC, VG, CK, CP, KS, JWF, and JWF1 denote the sweet cherry genetic maps reported in <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs11295-018-1252-2" title="High-density linkage maps constructed in sweet cherry (Prunus avium L.) using cross- and self-pollination populations reveal chromosomal homozygosity in inbred families and non-syntenic regions with the peach genome" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-7">Calle et al. (2018)</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0127750" title="Construction of high density sweet cherry (Prunus avium L.) linkage maps using microsatellite markers and SNPs detected by genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-18">Guajardo et al. (2015)</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0054743" title="Construction and comparative analyses of highly dense linkage maps of two sweet cherry intra-specific progenies of commercial cultivars" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Klagges et al. (2013)</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0048305" title="Development and evaluation of a genome-wide 6K SNP array for diploid sweet cherry and tetraploid sour cherry" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-36">Peace et al. (2012)</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al. (2017)</a> and <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0141261" title="Construction of commercial sweet cherry linkage maps and QTL analysis for trunk diameter" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-51">Wang et al. (2015)</a>, respectively.</span>
<div class="figcaption-footer">
<div class="article-image-download"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-1-full.png" class="btn btn-mini" download="peerj-9114-fig-1.png" itemprop="url"><i class="icon-large icon-picture"> </i> Download full-size image</a></div>
<div class="object-id article-component-doi">DOI: <a href="https://doi.org/10.7717/peerj.9114/fig-1" data-toggle="tooltip" title="Cite this object using this DOI">10.7717/peerj.9114/fig-1</a>
</div>
</div></figcaption></figure>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Annotation of repetitive sequences</h3>
<p id="p-22">The Repbase library and repetitive motifs were searched and 32.71% (over 91 MB) of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly was found to be repetitive. Different repetitive elements were annotated in sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome, and their distribution were shown in <a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-3" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-3">Table 3</a>. Long-terminal-repeat retrotransposons (6.39%) were predominant among the repetitive elements. The annotated repeat sequence length of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome was 28.4 MB shorter than the sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>), which may explain why the <i>k</i>-mer method estimated a smaller genome size for cv. Tieton than cv. Satonishiki (299.17 vs. 352.9 MB).</p>

<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">cDNA assembly and noncoding RNA (ncRNA) annotation</h3>
<p id="p-25">Trinity was used to assembly the high quality cDNA reads (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnbt.1883" title="Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-16">Grabherr et al., 2011</a>). A total of 33,401 transcripts with a total length of 42.6 MB were generated. The length of the assembled transcripts ranged from 201 to 15,591 nt, with a mean length of 1,276 nt. These assembled contigs were considered to be unigenes, and the distribution of their lengths is shown in <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-8" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-8">Table S7</a>.</p>
<p id="p-26">Noncoding RNA includes miRNA, rRNA, snoRNA, tRNA, and the tRNA pseudogene. A total of 109,277 ncRNAs were generated, with a total length of 7.35 MB, representing 2.63% of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome. As summarized in <a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-4" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-4">Table 4</a>, our annotation predicted fewer tRNAs and rRNAs, compared to the annotation in of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>).</p>

<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Protein-coding gene prediction and functional annotation</h3>
<p id="p-30">In total, 30,439 genes coding for 30,975 proteins were predicted in the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly. A summary of the predicted results using different methods was shown in <a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-5" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-5">Table 5</a>. The de novo methods predicted 47,866 gene models, but the average gene length was shorter than other methods. After correcting with the transcript evidence, more than 16,000 genes were filtered out.</p>

<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Gene family analysis compared with other plant species</h3>
<p id="p-36">OrthoFinder (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs13059-015-0721-2" title="OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-13">Emms &amp; Kelly, 2015</a>) identified the potential orthologous genes between the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome and the other 12 plant genomes. The results of gene orthologous analysis were shown in <a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-9" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-9">Table S8</a>. Gene family clustering identified 23,129 common orthogroups consisting of 375,493 genes (81.1% of the total genes) in these genomes. 8,465 orthogroups were present in all species, and 246 were single-copy genes. In the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome, 46 orthogroups (124 genes) were unique and 2,062 orphan genes were identified that could not be clustered with any genes in the thirteen genomes. A species tree was constructed using STRIDE (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fmolbev%2Fmsx259" title="STRIDE: species tree root inference from gene duplication events" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-14">Emms &amp; Kelly, 2017</a>), as part of OrthoFinder. As shown in <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-2" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-2">Fig. 2</a>, sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) exhibits a closer relationship with flowering cherry (<i>Prunus yedoensis</i>) than peach (<i>Prunus persica</i>) and Chinese plum (<i>Prunus mume</i>). A comparison was conducted to evaluate the expansion or contraction of these gene families using CAFÉ (version 4.2) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtl097" title="CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-11">De Bie et al., 2006</a>), and the results were shown in <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-2" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-2">Fig. 2</a>. A total of 1,012 gene families expanded and 3,642 gene families contracted in the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome compared to the other 12 plant genomes (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-2" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-2">Fig. 2</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig" itemprop="image" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject" id="fig-2"><div class="image-container"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-2-2x.jpg" title="View the full image" class="fresco" data-fresco-caption="Figure 2: Species tree and gene family expansion analysis of 13 plant species." data-fresco-group="figure" data-fresco-options="fit: 'width', ui: 'outside', thumbnails: false, loop: true, position: true, overflow: true, preload: false"><img class="graphic" src="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-2-1x.jpg" itemprop="contentUrl" sizes="(min-width: 1200px) 581px, (max-width: 1199px) and (min-width: 980px) 462px, (max-width: 979px) and (min-width: 768px) 347px, (max-width: 767px) calc(100vw - 50px)" srcset="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-2-2x.jpg 1200w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-2-1x.jpg 600w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-2-small.jpg 355w" data-image-id="fig-2" alt="Species tree and gene family expansion analysis of 13 plant species." data-full="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-2-full.png" data-thumb="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-2-thumb.jpg" data-original="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-2.png" data-image-type="figure" data-jats-mimetype="image" data-jats-mime-subtype="png" width="600" height="448"></a></div>
<figcaption itemprop="description">
<h4 class="heading">
<span class="caption-label">Figure 2: </span>Species tree and gene family expansion analysis of 13 plant species.</h4>
<span class="p">The species tree were constructed using STRIDE. Gene family expansions are indicated in red, and gene family contractions are indicated in green.</span>

</div>
</div></figcaption></figure>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Comparison between sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome and cv. Satonishiki genome</h3>
<p id="p-38">According to <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-3" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-3">Fig. 3A</a>, genomic analysis using D-GENIES showed a high scaffold-level synteny of the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome compared to sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome. High chromosome-level synteny was also detected in the two sets of pseudomolecules, except at the end of chromosomes 1, 4, 5, and 6 (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-3" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-3">Fig. 3B</a>). Based on <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-3" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-3">Fig. 3A</a>, the sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome assembly had a better contig contiguity, whereas the sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome was more fragmented.</p>
<figure class="fig" itemprop="image" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject" id="fig-3"><div class="image-container"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3-2x.jpg" title="View the full image" class="fresco" data-fresco-caption="Figure 3: Synteny analysis between sweet cherry (Prunus avium) cv. Tieton genome and cv. Satonishiki genome." data-fresco-group="figure" data-fresco-options="fit: 'width', ui: 'outside', thumbnails: false, loop: true, position: true, overflow: true, preload: false"><img class="graphic" src="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3-1x.jpg" itemprop="contentUrl" sizes="(min-width: 1200px) 581px, (max-width: 1199px) and (min-width: 980px) 462px, (max-width: 979px) and (min-width: 768px) 347px, (max-width: 767px) calc(100vw - 50px)" srcset="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3-2x.jpg 1200w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3-1x.jpg 600w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3-small.jpg 355w" data-image-id="fig-3" alt="Synteny analysis between sweet cherry (Prunus avium) cv. Tieton genome and cv. Satonishiki genome." data-full="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3-full.png" data-thumb="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3-thumb.jpg" data-original="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3.png" data-image-type="figure" data-jats-mimetype="image" data-jats-mime-subtype="png" width="600" height="277"></a></div>
<figcaption itemprop="description">
<h4 class="heading">
<span class="caption-label">Figure 3: </span>Synteny analysis between sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i>) cv. Tieton genome and cv. Satonishiki genome.</h4>
<span class="p">(A) Scaffold level synteny dot plot. (B) Chromosome-scale synteny dot plot. Sequence identity is indicated by colors.</span>
<div class="figcaption-footer">
<div class="article-image-download"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2020/9114/1/fig-3-full.png" class="btn btn-mini" download="peerj-9114-fig-3.png" itemprop="url"><i class="icon-large icon-picture"> </i> Download full-size image</a></div>
<div class="object-id article-component-doi">DOI: <a href="https://doi.org/10.7717/peerj.9114/fig-3" data-toggle="tooltip" title="Cite this object using this DOI">10.7717/peerj.9114/fig-3</a>
</div>
</div></figcaption></figure>
<p id="p-40">The original annotation of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fdnares%2Fdsx020" title="The genome sequence of sweet cherry (Prunus avium) for use in genomics-assisted breeding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Shirasawa et al., 2017</a>) and the re-annotated version of cv. Satonishiki genome released by the NCBI Eukaryotic Genome Annotation Pipeline were used to compare the gene content with our annotation of sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome. OrthoFinder analysis showed that the originally annotated version of cv. Satonishiki had 48 species-specific orthogroups represented 349 genes from our cv. Tieton genome annotation and the NCBI annotation of cv. Satonishiki genome (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-7" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-7">Table 7</a>). The original version of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki assembly annotated 41% more genes than our cv. Tieton genome annotation, however, the re-annotated version of cv. Satonishiki genome annotated a similar number of genes with our cv. Tieton genome. The increased gene numbers in the original annotation of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome can be attributed to the fragmentation of genes onto multiple individual contigs. The re-annotated version of sweet cherry cv. Satonishiki genome adopted RNA-seq to improve the quality of the gene annotation by connecting genes fragmented in the assembly process (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pcbi.1003998" title="Extensive error in the number of genes inferred from draft genome assemblies" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-12">Denton et al., 2014</a>). This method was also used in our sweet cherry cv. Tieton genome annotation process.</p>
<figure class="table-wrap" id="table-7"><div class="caption">

</section>
</section>
<section class="sec" id="conclusions">
<h2 class="heading">Conclusion</h2>
<p id="p-44">We successfully assembled a high-quality reference genome of sweet cherry cv. Tieton using linked reads sequencing technology. The assembly will be a valuable resource for future breeding efforts, gene function characterization and cultivar identification in the sweet cherry, as well as for comparative genomic analysis with other <i>Prunus</i> species.</p>
</section>

<h3 class="heading">Data Availability</h3>
<div class="fn" id="addinfo-1">
<p>The following information was supplied regarding data availability:</p>
</div>
<div class="fn" id="addinfo-2">
<p>Raw sequencing reads are available in GenBank under Bioproject ID <a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA503752">PRJNA503752</a>.</p>
<p>Genome assembly, annotation and chromosome-scale pseudomolecule construction date are available at Figshare: Wang, Jiawei; Liu, Weizhen; Zhu, Dongzi; Liu, Qingzhong (2020): A de novo assembly of the sweet cherry (<i>Prunus avium</i> cv. Tieton) genome using linked-read sequencing technology. figshare. Dataset. <a class="uri" href="https://dx.doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.9810236.v1">DOI 10.6084/m9.figshare.9810236.v1</a>. The genome assembly (whole genome shotgun sequencing project) is available at NCBI: <a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/VTVB00000000">VTVB00000000</a>.</p>
</div>

<script>
    (function(){
        $('#download-subscribe-submit').click(function(){

            var button = $(this);
            var url = button.data('url');
            if(button.attr('disabled')) return;

            $.get(url, function(response){

                if(!response.token){
                    errorContainer.html('Server error, you have not been subscribed').show();
                    button.html('Subscribe').removeAttr('disabled');
                    return;
                }

                var errorContainer = $('#download-subscribe-error-container');
                errorContainer.html('').hide();
                button.html('<i class="icon-spin icon-spinner"></i>').attr('disabled', true);

                var signedIn = button.data('signed-in');
                var sectionName = button.data('section-name');
                var data = {
                    _token: response.token
                };

                if(!signedIn) {
                    var email = $('#download-subscribe-email').val();
                    data.email = email;
                    data.freq = $('download-subscribe-freq').val();
                }

                $.ajax({
                    url: url,
                    method: 'POST',
                    data: data
                }).success(function(response){
                    button.hide();
                    $('.js-close-download-modal').trigger('click');

                    PeerJ.Tools.ToastNotifications.add({
                        type: 'success',
                        title: 'Subscribed',
                        text: sectionName ? 'You subscribed to ' + sectionName : 'You subscribed to this article\'s subjects'
                    });

                }).error(function(response){
                    if(response.responseJSON && response.responseJSON.errors){
                        errorContainer.html(response.responseJSON.errors[0]).show();
                    }
                }).complete(function(){
                    button.html('Subscribe').removeAttr('disabled');
                });

            });
        });

    }());
</script>

                <script>
            (function(){

                function initTweets(){
                    //Get count
                    var tweetCountUrl = '/articles/9114/tweet-count/';
                    $.get(tweetCountUrl, function(response){
                        if(response.count){
                            $('#tweet-count-container').html('View ' + response.count + ' tweet' + (response.count > 1 ? 's' : ''));
                            $('#btn-view-tweets').fadeIn(200);
                        }
                    });

                    //Get first page
                    $('.tweet-items').html('Loading...');
                    var tweetsPageUrl = '/articles/9114/tweets/';
                    $.get(tweetsPageUrl, function(response){
                        if(response.html){
                            $('.tweet-items').html(response.html);
                            $("html, body").animate({scrollTop: 0}, 500);
                        }
                    });
                }

                setTimeout(initTweets, 2500);

            })();
        </script>

                
        <script>
            $(function () {
                // syntax highlighting for code blocks
                $("pre > code").each(function() {
                    var node = $(this);

                    var language;

                    // JATS >=1.1
                    language = node.data('jats-language');

                    if (!language) {
                        // JATS <1.1
                        language = node.data('jats-preformat-type');

                        // ignore default 'code' type
                        if (language === 'code') {
                            language = null;
                        }
                    }

                    if (language) {
                        node.addClass('language-' + language);
                    }

                    hljs.highlightBlock(this);
                });
            });
        </script>
    <!--<![endif]-->
